## 26.40 — Football as Haiti's Dominant Cultural Sport

📚 Football (soccer) is Haiti's dominant sport by coverage, conversation volume, and
national emotional investment. Three sports media sources — Haiti-Tempo, FHF, and
Totalmix Radio — all confirm football's primacy, with basketball as a secondary sport
and all others at significant distance.

📚 The domestic league runs three tiers: **D1** (top division), **D2**, and **D3**.
Beyond domestic football, Haiti fields national selections at Senior/A, U-23, U-20,
U-17, and Women's levels. Haitian expatriate players in European and North American
clubs are tracked weekly by dedicated media.

📚 The men's national team is called **Les Grenadiers**. The battle cry is
**"Grenadye Alaso!"** Team colors: bleu et rouge (blue and red). The women's
national team is called **Les Grenadières** — the feminine form, used consistently
in all three sources.

📚 **Three sports media institutions cover Haitian football:**
- **Haiti-Tempo** (haititempo.com) — active Haitian outlet, website-only
- **FHF** (fhf.ht) — official federation, nearly dormant (3 articles total, last June 2024)
- **Totalmix Radio** (totalmixradio.com) — Miami-based, radio + TV + app, 682+ pages archived, most comprehensive

📚 A structural pattern repeats across all three: the most active and comprehensive
Haitian sports institution (Totalmix) operates from outside Haiti. The federation
(FHF) has a nearly dead website. Private media (Haiti-Tempo, Totalmix) carry the
informational load. This mirrors the broader institutional pattern already in the corpus.

---

## 26.41 — Haiti at the 2026 FIFA World Cup

📚 **Haiti has qualified for the 2026 FIFA World Cup**, hosted across the United
States, Canada, and Mexico. This is among the most significant moments in Haitian
football history. The qualification was earned through real CONCACAF competition —
not automatic entry. Haiti beat Saint Lucia 2-1 (June 2024) in qualifying. The
campaign was genuine.

📚 **Coach: Sébastien Migné**, French, 53 years old. Full authority over selections.
His choices have generated significant public debate (see 26.43).

📚 **Squad of 26 players** announced May 15, 2026. Two preparatory friendlies
against **New Zealand** and **Peru** scheduled for early June 2026, before the
tournament opens.

📚 **FHF created a new Département du Football Jeune** (2026) — confirmed via
Totalmix. Institutional development happening alongside World Cup preparation.

📚 **Totalmix headline context (May 2026):** The squad announcement and surrounding
debate dominated Haitian football media. The D1 final (Baltimore SC vs Violette AC)
is running simultaneously — two major national football events at once.

---

## 26.42 — The Official World Cup Squad (26 Players)

📚 **Coach: Sébastien Migné** | Announced: May 15, 2026

**Gardiens / Goalkeepers:**
Johny Placide · Alexandre Pierre · Josué Duverger

**Défenseurs / Defenders:**
Carlens Arcus · Wilguens Paugain · Ricardo Adé · Jean Kévin Duverne
Hannes Delcroix · Keeto Thermoncy · Martin Expérience · Duke Lacroix

**Milieux / Midfielders:**
Dominique Simon · Leverton Pierre · Woodensky Pierre · Carl Fred Sainté
Danley Jean-Jacques · Josué Casimir · Jean Ricner Bellegarde

**Attaquants / Forwards:**
Duckens Nazon · Frantzdy Pierrot · Deedson Louicius · Ruben Providence
Yassin Fortuné · Wilson Isidor · Lenny Joseph · Derrick Etienne Jr.

📚 **Returners from injury:** Johny Placide, Carlens Arcus.
📚 **Surprise inclusions:** Dominique Simon (MF), Lenny Joseph (FW) — first World Cup call-ups.
📚 **Notable omissions:** Dany Jean (most discussed), Miki Cantave, Fafa Picault, Fabrice Picault.

---

## 26.43 — Squad Debate: The Omission of Dany Jean

📚 The omission of **Dany Jean** (23 years old, 19 caps, SCU Torreense / Portugal D2)
was the most discussed selection controversy. He had 6 goals and 3 assists in the
current season, helping his club push for promotion. Haiti-Tempo called him "le grand
oublié" — the great forgotten one.

📚 Competition for his wing position was severe: Josué Casimir, Ruben Providence,
Yassin Fortuné, and Deedson Louicius all compete there. Derrick Étienne Jr. was
preferred. Dany Jean was rarely used by Migné during qualifying (one partial appearance).
His strong club form drove fan expectations; the omission felt disproportionate to them.

📚 Migné's selection also dropped Fabrice Picault (started vs Saint Lucia in June 2024
qualifying), Miki Cantave, and Fafa Picault without significant public debate around
those omissions — Dany Jean absorbed most of the reaction.

---

## 26.44 — Expatriate Players: The Structural Diaspora Link

📚 The vast majority of the 26 World Cup players are based in European or North American
clubs. Woodensky Pierre (Violette AC) is the only domestically-based player in the squad.
Haiti's national team structurally depends on its diaspora.

📚 **Weekly-tracked expatriates (from Totalmix, recent results):**

| Player | Club | Country | Recent |
|---|---|---|---|
| Frantzdy Pierrot | Çaykur Rizespor | Turkey D1 | Joined Feb 2026 |
| Yassin Fortuné | FC Vizela | Portugal | Goal scored March 2026 |
| Ruben Providence | Almere City FC | Netherlands | Goal + assist March 2026 |
| Wilson Isidor | FC Lugano | Switzerland | Loss 1-3 March 2026 |
| Dany Jean | SCU Torreense | Portugal D2 | Contract signed March 2026 |
| Alexandre Pierre | Châteauroux | France | Promoted to Ligue 2 May 15, 2026 |
| Emerson Laissé | — | — | Reportedly tracked by Real Madrid (unverified) |

📚 **Alexandre Pierre double moment (May 15, 2026):** Selected for the World Cup and
his club promoted to Ligue 2 on the same day. Haiti-Tempo covered it as a single story.

📚 **Emerson Laissé:** Young Haitian forward reportedly of interest to Real Madrid
per Italian journalist Matteo Moretto. If confirmed, would be the highest-profile
Haitian men's signing in history. Status unverified.

📚 **Pattern:** Men's players cluster in lower European leagues (Portugal D2, Swiss
league, Dutch second tier, Turkish D1, French lower divisions). The gap between
men's club placements and Dumornay's OL Lyon is structural, not individual.

---

## 26.45 — Woodensky Pierre and the Meaning of the D1

📚 Woodensky Pierre (midfielder, Violette Athletic Club) is the **only D1-based player**
in the 26-man World Cup squad. Haiti-Tempo framed his selection as "la fierté de la D1
Haïtienne" — pride of the Haitian D1. This framing reveals a structural gap: a domestic
player making the national squad is exceptional enough to be a news story on its own.

📚 Violette AC is simultaneously in the D1 Paryaj Lakay final (vs Baltimore SC, first
leg played May 4, return leg pending). Woodensky Pierre's club and his national team
are both at maximum-stakes moments simultaneously.

---

## 26.46 — FHF: Institutional Profile and Founding History

📚 The **Fédération Haïtienne de Football (FHF)** is Haiti's official football governing
body. Founded **1904** — one of the oldest in the Caribbean, born from the "Commission
de Football de l'Union des Sociétés Sportives Haïtiennes." Recognized as a public
utility institution by presidential decree on **April 4, 1952.**

📚 FHF mission: regulate, supervise, and promote football nationally. Four stated values:
respect, excellence, good governance (*bonne gouvernance*), pleasure (*le plaisir*).
Officially apolitical, non-religious, anti-discrimination.

📚 **Institutional communication reality:** The FHF website has published only 3 articles
total, the most recent dated June 8, 2024 — nearly a year before the World Cup squad
announcement. The official federation is essentially silent as Haiti enters its biggest
tournament. Private media (Haiti-Tempo, Totalmix) carry all active communication.

---

## 26.47 — FHF Governance Crisis: FIFA Normalization Committee

📚 On **December 11, 2020**, FIFA's Bureau placed the FHF under a **Comité de
Normalisation** — an externally-appointed committee replacing elected FHF leadership,
jointly named by FIFA and CONCACAF. Trigger: governance failure within the FHF.

📚 **Committee mandate:** Manage FHF affairs, revise statutes, organize elections for
a new executive committee, ensure financial transition.

📚 **Members listed on FHF site:**
- Présidente: Monique ANDRE
- Membre: Yvon SEVERE
- Membre: Gally AMAZAN

📚 **The mandate was set to expire November 2025.** Whether elections occurred or the
normalization situation was resolved before the World Cup is not confirmed in any source
reviewed. Haiti may be entering the 2026 World Cup still under FIFA-managed governance.
The FHF self-documents this on its homepage without apparent awareness of how it reads.

---

## 26.48 — Stade Sylvio Cator

📚 Haiti's primary national football stadium, located in Port-au-Prince. Named after
**Sylvio Cator**, Haitian athlete and 1928 Olympic long jump competitor. Venue for
national team matches, major cup competitions, and landmark events in Haitian football.

📚 **Historical uses documented:** First women's match at the stadium (1973); hosted
the 1991 CONCACAF Women's Championship. Appears modest in size from FHF site imagery.
Capacity and current condition need external verification.

---

## 26.49 — Women's Football in Haiti: A History Since 1971

📚 Organized women's football in Haiti began **December 19, 1971** — over 50 years
of continuous history. The FHF documents this in detail and explicitly frames it as
part of Haitian women's emancipation.

📚 **Origin:** The Georges brothers (Garry and Phèdre) organized the first match at
Parc Sainte-Thérèse de Pétion-Ville — Sacré-Cœur vs Canado Haïtien. At the time,
football was considered exclusively male. The match triggered a wave of women's clubs
within a year: Amazones, Tigresses, Gladiatrice, Excelsior, and others.

📚 **Timeline:**

| Year | Milestone |
|---|---|
| 1971 | First women's match — Pétion-Ville |
| 1972 | Commission de football féminin created |
| 1973 | First match at Stade Sylvio Cator |
| 1975 | First international match; FHF affiliation |
| 1987 | Ligue Haïtienne de Football Féminin; first national championship |
| 1991 | Haiti hosts CONCACAF Women's Championship at Stade Sylvio Cator |
| 2018 | Women's U-20 at FIFA U-20 Women's World Cup, France |
| 2021 | FHF celebrates 50th anniversary of first match |
| 2022 | FHF designates "Year of the Cinquantenaire du football féminin haïtien" |
| 2023 | Senior women's team qualifies for FIFA Women's World Cup (Australia/NZ) |

📚 The FHF frames the 1971 match as an "élément déclencheur" for women's emancipation.
Haiti's women's football program has exported players internationally for decades.

---

## 26.50 — Youth Development Pipeline

📚 Haiti maintains youth selections at U-17, U-20, U-23, and Women's youth levels.
The competitive pathway runs through CONCACAF youth championships.

📚 **Dominican Republic as preparation base:** Haiti's youth national teams train in
Santo Domingo when preparing for CONCACAF competition. Confirmed for U17 (January–
February 2023, CONCACAF U17 Championship preparation). Domestic facilities or security
conditions make it difficult to prepare at home. Haiti's football program depends on a
country with hostile border politics for its training infrastructure.

📚 **U17 coach (2023): Angelo Jean Baptiste.** Playing philosophy: rhythm, cohesion,
offensive principles, ball control, vision, anticipation. Players trained at Parque
del Este, Santo Domingo. Players mentioned: Bryan Destin.

📚 **Women's U-17 (2026):** Lost 3-1 to Puerto Rico in World Cup qualifying (March 2026).
Competed in Costa Rica tournament (ended). Active pipeline despite result.

📚 **Women's U-20 (2018):** Competed at FIFA Women's U-20 World Cup in France.

---

## 26.51 — D1 Paryaj Lakay: League Structure and Current Season

📚 **Full commercial name: D1 Paryaj Lakay** — "Paryaj Lakay" roughly translates as
"bet at home." This is a sponsor name for the top division, confirming commercial
partnership exists in Haitian domestic football. The competition organizer is the
**LHF (Ligue Haïtienne de Football)** — distinct from the FHF (federation).

📚 **Season structure:**
Série d'ouverture → Série de clôture → Playoffs (knockout) → Final (two-legged)

📚 **2025–2026 season — live status (May 2026):**
- **Final:** Baltimore SC vs Violette AC
- First leg: May 4, 2026 — 0-0 draw (Parc Félix Sévère)
- Return leg: pending. Everything to play for.

📚 **Playoff qualifiers (série de clôture final standings):**
1. Tempête FC — 13 pts
2. FICA — 13 pts
3. Racing FC — 12 pts
4. Baltimore SC — 12 pts

Violette AC qualified through Zone Sud. Both clubs advanced to the final.

📚 **FICA sanction:** Violent incidents at Parc Saint-Victor during FICA vs Baltimore SC
semifinal return. LHF issued heavy sanctions against FICA. Baltimore advanced.

📚 **Match venues confirm national geography:**
Parc Sainte-Thérèse (Pétion-Ville), Parc Notre-Dame (Ouanaminthe, far north),
Parc Bayas (Mirebalais, central), Centre FIFA-Goal (Croix-des-Bouquets), Parc Saint-Yves.
D1 is a genuinely national competition — not a Port-au-Prince league.

---

## 26.52 — Key D1 Clubs: Identities, Geography, History

📚 **Baltimore SC — "Les Orioles" / "Les Saint-Marcois"**
From Saint-Marc (Artibonite region). Founded ~1980. Current finalists. Has experienced
internal governance crises (2024). Has signed Argentine players in prior seasons.
Last-minute qualifier vs Ouanaminthe FC (85' goal, Alexandre Rempart) secured playoff spot.

📚 **Violette Athletic Club — "Le Vieux Tigre" (The Old Tiger)**
Port-au-Prince. Founded ~1918 — Haiti's oldest major club (100+ years). Won CONCACAF
Club Champions in **1984** — the only Haitian club to win that competition. Regular
CONCACAF Champions League participant. CFU Club Championship semi-finalist (2022).
Woodensky Pierre (World Cup) plays here.

📚 **Tempête FC**
From Saint-Marc — same city as Baltimore SC. Derby relationship. Série de clôture
playoff qualifier (13 pts).

📚 **FICA — Football Inter Club Association**
Reached playoff semifinals before violent incident. Heavily sanctioned by LHF.
Northern Haiti geography.

📚 **Racing FC des Gonaïves**
From Gonaïves (Artibonite). Founded ~1962 — 64 years of history. Dedicated historical
profile published April 2026. Playoff qualifier (12 pts).

📚 **AS Capoise — "Le Vieux Coq Capois" (The Old Cock of the Cap)**
From Cap-Haïtien (north, Haiti's second city). Civic pride of Cap-Haïtien embedded
in the club nickname. Regular playoff contender.

📚 **Real Hope FA**
Active in playoffs. Participated in CONCACAF Champions League 2026 — lost to LA Galaxy
3-0. Confirms a Haitian club is in the 2026 CONCACAF CL.

📚 **Arcahaie FC**
From Arcahaie (near Port-au-Prince). Reached CONCACAF League round of 16 (2021).
Involved in the violent 2021 D1 final.

📚 **Other active D1 clubs:** Don Bosco FC, America FC des Cayes (south), Ouanaminthe
FC (north), FC Juventus, AS Mirebalais (central), Cosmopolites.

📚 **Geographic map of D1:**
- North: AS Capoise, Ouanaminthe FC
- Artibonite: Baltimore SC, Tempête FC, Racing Gonaïves
- Port-au-Prince area: Violette AC, FICA, Don Bosco, Arcahaie FC
- Central: AS Mirebalais
- South: America FC des Cayes

D1 covers the whole country. Club identity likely carries regional/civic meaning.

---

## 26.53 — Violence at Haitian Football Matches

📚 Violence at D1 matches is a documented recurring pattern, not an exception.
Two confirmed incidents from sources reviewed:

📚 **January 2021 — D1 Final (Violette AC vs Arcahaie FC, Parc Saint-Yves):**
Match abandoned at 1-1 after a riot. At least one person killed. A bus burned.
A journalist injured. Match replayed at neutral venue (Parc Bayas, Mirebalais) —
Violette AC won. This followed the 2-1 first-leg win by Violette at Centre FIFA-Goal.

📚 **May 2026 — D1 Semifinal (FICA vs Baltimore SC, Parc Saint-Victor):**
Violent incidents during the return leg. LHF sanctioned FICA heavily. Baltimore advanced.

📚 **Pattern:** Both incidents at maximum-stakes knockout moments (a final, a semifinal).
Violence concentrates where pressure peaks. The same absence of security infrastructure
that governs all public space in Haiti applies to stadiums.

📚 **Intersection with existing corpus:** Football matches are among the few large-scale
public gatherings in Haiti. The insecurity context — gang territory, absence of crowd
control, personal security calculation for all public movement — does not stop at the
stadium gate.

---

## 26.54 — Totalmix Radio: Haiti's Primary Sports Media Institution

📚 **Totalmix Radio** — "The First Haitian Sports Network." Miami-based. Founded by
Gaby Joseph. French-language. The most comprehensive Haitian sports outlet by volume.

📚 **Platform:** Live radio stream, two TV channels (TotalMix TV 1, TotalMix TV 2),
website (682+ archived article pages), app, active on Facebook / Twitter (@TOTALMIXRADIO,
@TOTALMIXTV) / YouTube / Instagram.

📚 **Programming structure:** Multiple news bulletins daily — Premier Bulletin, Deuxième,
Cinquième, Sixième — plus specialist shows: Bon Pieds Bon Oeil (football analysis),
Bwasé Boul (football discussion — "kick the ball"), Explo Sport, MegaSport, Gaby Live,
Compas Total, Sportif Ibo, Platfom Magik (Chak Lendi — every Monday), Totalmix Rapp.

📚 **Sports covered:** Football (men local, men expats, women local, women expats,
amputee), Basketball (men, women, expats), Tennis, Tennis de table, Athlétisme, Boxe,
Cyclisme, Judo, Karaté, Natation, Volleyball.

📚 **Structural note:** The most active Haitian sports institution is Miami-based.
It operates from the diaspora to serve Haiti. This is the same pattern as FHF governance
failure, youth team preparation in the DR, and most World Cup players living abroad.

---

## 26.55 — Melchie Daëlle Dumornay: Haiti's Biggest Active Sports Star

📚 **Melchie Daëlle Dumornay** — also referred to as "Corventina Dumornay." Age 21
(as of 2025/26). Midfielder/forward. Plays for **OL Lyon** (France) — the world's
top women's club.

📚 **2025-26 season at OL Lyon:** 22 goals (including 6 in Champions League) + 9 assists
= contribution to 31 goals. Best player at OL Lyon this season per Totalmix. Propelled
OL Lyon to the **Women's Champions League final (2026)**. Won the **Coupe LFFP** (French
women's cup) with OL Lyon.

📚 **Global standing:** ESPN ranked her #9 among world's best players under 21. First
**Ballon d'Or nomination (2025)** widely anticipated — Totalmix called her the clear
frontrunner at OL Lyon. Previously played for Stade de Reims (France) before joining OL.

📚 **2023 Women's World Cup (Australia/New Zealand):** Haiti qualified — historic
achievement. Dumornay was the team's star. FIFA Secretary General featured her in a
viral video. She said: the World Cup was "un rêve qui est devenu réalité." She cited
collective sacrifice over years. The perception of women's football in Haiti improved
markedly with the World Cup, per her own words in that video.

📚 **Why this matters for TwinZile:** Dumornay is operating at the top of her sport
globally while representing Haiti as her primary identity. This is not a diaspora story
where a player chose another country — she plays *for Haiti*. No other active Haitian
athlete in any sport is at a comparable global level. She is in a category of her own
in Haitian sports history.

📚 **Captain of the Grenadières:** Nérilia Mondésir (OL Reign, NWSL, USA). Mondésir
is an established star; Dumornay is the rising face of the program.

---

## 26.56 — Les Grenadières: Women's National Team

📚 Haiti's women's national team. Coach (2025–26): **Malou Quignette** (French).

📚 **Active competitive targets:**
- **CONCACAF W Championship 2026** — Haiti qualifying now (vs Mexico noted). This
  championship determines qualification for the **Women's World Cup 2027 (Brazil)**
  and the **2028 Los Angeles Olympics**.
- **Women's World Cup 2027 squad building** already underway (April 2026).

📚 **Key players:**

| Player | Club | Role |
|---|---|---|
| Nérilia Mondésir | OL Reign (NWSL) | Captain, Forward |
| Melchie Daëlle Dumornay | OL Lyon (France) | Forward/MF, star |
| Batcheba Louis | French league | Forward |
| Sherly Jeudy | French league | Midfielder |
| Roselord Borgella | — | Midfielder |
| Kerly Théus | — | Goalkeeper |
| Kethna Louis | — | Defender |
| Amandine Pierre-Louis | — | Defender |
| Anyssa Ibrahim | Portugal | Forward |
| Chelsea Surpris | — | Defender |
| Tabita Dougenie Joseph | OL Marseille | — |
| Maudeline Moryl | — | Midfielder |

📚 **CFU youth dominance:** Haiti has dominated Caribbean youth women's football —
beating Puerto Rico, Jamaica, Dominican Republic, Grenada in CFU tournaments repeatedly.
Named players from those generations (Mondésir, Dumornay, Estericove Joseph, Flero
Dina Surpris, Abaïna Louis) have risen to professional level abroad.

📚 **Structural comparison:** The Grenadières reached a World Cup (2023) before the
Grenadiers (men, 2026). Haiti's women's program has been the more consistently
successful international program in recent years. This is a fact worth holding for
how personas might frame national pride in sports.

---

## 26.57 — Beyond Football: Haiti's Broader Sports Landscape

📚 The following sports have organized competition beyond football, confirmed by
Totalmix Radio coverage categories and article snippets:

**Basketball:**
Men's and women's U23 national teams active (competed abroad, 2026). NBA covered
as expatriate interest content. Basket interscolaire (school league) has its own
competition. Basketball is the clearest second sport after football.

**Volleyball:**
Dream Team Volleyball Club (DTVC) active — won double in men's competition.
Haiti beach volleyball competed at Cayman Islands tournament (16th place).
Cap-Haïtien has its own regional volleyball league (ambassador named 2026).
Volleyball has genuine organizational structure at regional level.

**Other organized sports:**
Boxing, Athletics (Athlétisme), Judo, Karaté, Cycling (Cyclisme), Swimming
(Natation), Tennis, Table Tennis — all have Totalmix coverage categories, implying
active competition. Whether each has a national federation is not confirmed.

**Football des Amputés:**
Haiti has an amputee football program, listed as a dedicated category on both
Haiti-Tempo and Totalmix. Given the level of gang violence and civilian injuries
in Haiti, this category may carry particular social weight.

📚 **Government sports ministry:** Ministère de la Jeunesse, des Sports et de
l'Action Civique. Current minister: **M. Pythagore Dumas** (newly appointed, 2026).

📚 **Université Quisqueya** launched two new sport/wellness initiatives (March 2026)
— sports entering academic institutional discourse.

---

| Dimension | Haiti-Tempo | FHF | Totalmix |
|---|---|---|---|
| Recency | Very current (May 2026) | Dormant (June 2024) | Very current + 682+ archived pages |
| World Cup squad | Full list, debate, analysis | Qualifying context only | Squad + expat tracking |
| D1 league | Basic | Nothing | Deep — clubs, results, history, drama |
| Club histories | Minimal | Nothing | Violette 1918, Baltimore ~1980, Racing 1962 |
| Women's football | Category listed | Detailed 50-year history | Grenadières, Dumornay, weekly results |
| Institutional history | Nothing | Founded 1904, 1952 decree | Nothing |
| Governance crisis | Nothing | Self-documents FIFA takeover | Nothing |
| Stade Sylvio Cator | Referenced | Named + historical uses | Referenced |
| Other sports | Listed | Nothing | Basketball, volleyball, all covered |
| Broadcasting model | Website only | Website only | Radio + TV + app + social |
| Geographic base | Haiti | Haiti | Miami (diaspora) |

## 26.60 — Qualifying Context: The Campaign Was Real

📚 From FHF article (June 8, 2024): Haiti beat **Saint Lucia 2-1** during CONCACAF
World Cup qualifying. The team continued preparation immediately after. This is not the
only qualifying match — it is one documented moment from a full campaign.

📚 **Qualifying-era squad vs. 2026 final squad — continuity and cuts:**
Players from June 2024 who made the 2026 squad: Duckens Nazon, Leverton Pierre,
Duke Lacroix, Jean Kévin Duverne, Derrick Étienne, Frantzdy Pierrot, Johny Placide,
Danley Jean-Jacques, Carl Fred Sainté, Deedson Louicius, Alexandre Pierre.

Players from June 2024 not in final 2026 squad: Belmar Joseph Jr, Bryan Labissiere,
Christopher Attys, François Dulysse, Garven Metusala, Mondy Prunier, Fabrice Picault.

📚 **Fabrice Picault** started vs Saint Lucia (played heavy minutes). Not in 2026
squad. His omission generated no significant public debate — Dany Jean drew all attention.

## 26.62 — Consolidated Validation Gaps: Sports Domain

All items below are 🔲 — require Dulex validation before any persona uses this material.

**Football — consumption and access:**
🔲 How do personas consume football — radio, phone streaming, communal TV, live attendance?
🔲 Does Marie-Ange's market pause for important matches?
🔲 Does gang territorial control in Cité Soleil affect Jèftè's ability to watch matches with others?

🔲 Does Stade Sylvio Cator attendance have a class dimension — who actually goes?

🔲 Are "Bon Pieds Bon Oeil" or "Bwasé Boul" reference shows that personas would name?

**World Cup:**
🔲 Emotional register of Haiti going to the World Cup for each persona given the crisis context — pure joy, bittersweet, or complicated?
🔲 Does World Cup fever create any behavioral shift — unusual spending, gathering despite insecurity?
🔲 Where do people watch World Cup matches in Port-au-Prince? Does it vary by neighborhood?
🔲 How does Sophonie (Miami) experience the World Cup in the host country?
🔲 Were there specific qualifying matches that became collective memory / koupe lage moments?
🔲 Is "Grenadye Alaso!" used conversationally during World Cup season, or is it media/stadium register only?
🔲 Does Kenzy use it sincerely, ironically, or both?

**Squad debates:**
🔲 Is the Dany Jean omission circulating as koupe lage on TikTok and WhatsApp?
🔲 Does fan critique of Migné map to *teyat* vocabulary, or does football occupy a separate emotional register from political critique?

**D1 and club identity:**
🔲 Does Jèftè support Violette AC? Or another Port-au-Prince club?
🔲 Does Roseline follow AS Capoise as her local club?
🔲 Does Dieunel follow Baltimore SC, Tempête FC, or Racing Gonaïves?
🔲 Is D1 club allegiance tied to city/regional identity the way it is in European football?
🔲 Does the D1 Paryaj Lakay final register as a major event for non-football-specialist personas?
🔲 Does match violence affect whether personas attend games at all?
🔲 Is stadium violence processed through the same insecurity lens as street violence?

**FHF governance:**
🔲 Is the FIFA Normalization Committee known to football fans?
🔲 Does FHF institutional failure map to the *teyat* register, or is football governance held separately?

**Women's football and Dumornay:**
🔲 Do Kenzy and Jèftè follow Dumornay via TikTok highlights?
🔲 Is Dumornay a cross-gender national pride reference, or primarily followed by women?
🔲 Do Nadège and Marilène have a specific relationship to the Grenadières?
🔲 Does the 2023 Women's World Cup register as a proud community memory?
🔲 Is the 50-year women's football history common knowledge or specialist knowledge inside Haiti?
🔲 Does Marie-Ange have any relationship to women's football as a cultural reference?

**Youth pipeline and DR dependency:**
🔲 Is it emotionally complicated that Haiti's youth teams prepare in the Dominican Republic given Haiti-DR border politics?
🔲 Do youth football academies exist inside Haiti and are they accessible to working-class children?

**Other sports:**
🔲 Basketball — does anyone in the persona set follow it (NBA clips on TikTok? local school leagues?)?
🔲 Volleyball — is it present in Roseline's Cap-Haïtien context?
🔲 Does Football des Amputés have any resonance given the injury context in Haiti?
🔲 Is the sports ministry (Pythagore Dumas) seen as functional or as another *teyat*?

## 26.63 — Klēb K-Fé: Site Profile

📚 **Klēb K-Fé** (klebkfe.com) — "Empowering the Haitian Music Culture with
Data-Driven Conversations." Diaspora-based Haitian music outlet, primary writer
Jean Gustave. Published in English — diaspora-oriented audience.

📚 **Content categories:** Konpa, Rap Kreyòl, Album Review, Business Music,
Community, Immigration, Political Klēb, Spotlight, Bridge (Haiti ↔ diaspora
content), Tech, Announcement.

📚 **The "Bridge" category** is significant: it explicitly frames content as
connecting Haiti and the diaspora. Haitian music is understood here as a
transnational cultural system, not a locally bounded one.

📚 **Klēb K-Fé uses iTunes worldwide charts as its primary industry metric.**
This reveals something structural: Haitian music audiences globally — diaspora
plus Haiti — are large enough to move international digital charts. Haitian
music has a worldwide commercial footprint.

📚 **Commercial infrastructure on the site:** Ding mobile recharge partnership
(send airtime minutes to Haiti). Sponsor code KLEBKFE = 75% off first recharge.
This placement is telling: a Haitian music site monetizes partly through enabling
diaspora-to-Haiti phone credit transfers. Community need and commercial opportunity
fused in the same ad.

---

## 26.64 — Konpa (Kompa): Haiti's Dominant Music Genre

📚 **Konpa** (also spelled Kompa; full name Konpa Dirèk) is Haiti's primary popular
music genre and the dominant sound of Haitian cultural identity. It is both a
dance genre and an emotional register — simultaneously a dancefloor vehicle and
a vehicle for lyrical depth.

📚 **Musical signature:** Rich, pulsing bass; rhythmic Konpa groove; emotive lead
vocals; keyboard solos (a defining instrument of the genre). The keyboard solo
is not decoration — it is identity. A band's keyboard player carries the band's
sonic fingerprint.

📚 **Dual function — confirmed by Klēb K-Fé:**
Konpa is "both light-hearted and soulful — a duality." It works for parties
and for intimate conversations under the same night sky. This is not a genre
that separates celebration from feeling. They are the same thing in Konpa.

📚 **"Che Dèv"** — Haitian Kreyòl for "masterpiece." T-Vice named their 2025
summer single this, framing romantic love as artistic creation. The word carries
aesthetic weight beyond its Konpa use.

📚 **Geographic reach:** Konpa rotates on radios, playlists, and venues across
the Haitian diaspora globally — Miami, New York, Boston, Montreal — not just
inside Haiti. It is the sonic thread connecting the diaspora to home.

📚 **iTunes as measure:** Multiple Konpa albums chart on the iTunes worldwide
chart simultaneously, competing directly with global releases. This is not a
niche genre by digital metrics.

📚 **Konpa concert circuit:** Major Haitian bands perform at arenas and venues
across the diaspora — Amerant Bank Arena (Florida), and similar large venues
in diaspora cities. Concert attendance is a significant social event for
diaspora communities.

---

## 26.65 — Key Konpa Artists and Current Landscape (2023–2025)

📚 **T-Vice**
One of the most enduring Konpa bands. "Long synonymous with Haitian kompa."
Active as of June 2025 — released "Che Dèv" (June 12, 2025, 40,000 YouTube
views in first 24 hours). Lead singer: Roberto. Keyboards: Reynaldo — described
as having a "distinct musical signature." Golden era Konpa sound, evolving.
Music videos shot in high production quality (LA-style, cinematic framing).
T-Vice's decade-spanning relevance makes them a reference point for what Konpa
endurance looks like.

📚 **CARIMI**
"The heartbeat of modern Haitian music for over two decades." Shaped a generation
of diaspora Haitians. Completed their final U.S. show March 21, 2025 at Amerant
Bank Arena, Sunrise, Florida — described as "closing a chapter in Kompa history."
Known anthems: "Ayiti Bang Bang," "Fanm sa Move." CARIMI's farewell is a genuine
cultural marker — their music defined diaspora identity for a generation.

📚 **Klass**
Album "My Pain Killer" (November 13, 2024) debuted at #1 on iTunes worldwide
chart. Active major band, current chart leader at time of review.

📚 **Fatima**
Female artist. Album "Pwoteje M" (12 songs, 44 min, November 11, 2024) debuted
at #2 on iTunes worldwide chart. Significant female presence in a male-dominated
genre. Album title translates as "Protect Me" — spiritually resonant framing.

📚 **Zafèm**
Album "LAS" (originally released May 5, 2023) resurfaced to #3 on iTunes
worldwide chart in November 2024 — 18 months after original release. Demonstrates
Konpa catalog longevity: great Konpa does not expire. Fans return to it.

📚 **Rebecca Zama**
Rising female voice. Single "Game Over" (August 2025) — described as "Kompa Love
vibes," Caribbean-powered anthem that flips heartbreak into a dancefloor track.
Positioned as "one of the most exciting Caribbean voices."

📚 **Industry dynamic:** As of November 2024, Klass (#1), Fatima (#2), and Zafèm
(#3) occupied the top three spots on the iTunes worldwide Haitian chart
simultaneously — three different artist profiles competing at the top. The chart
is competitive and diverse.

---

## 26.66 — Rap Kreyòl: The Second Genre

📚 **Rap Kreyòl** is Haitian rap performed in Haitian Kreyòl. It is the second
major Haitian music genre — distinct from Konpa in audience profile, social
function, and cultural register.

📚 **The recurring debate — "Rap Kreyòl is dead":**
This phrase has "echoed through social media timelines, X posts, and Instagram
comment sections like a haunting refrain for years." It is "more than an opinion —
it's been cemented into the psyche of the Haitian rap community, repeated by
insiders and veterans alike."

📚 **The counter-position — confirmed by Klēb K-Fé:**
"Rap Kreyòl is not dead. It's just not being pushed by the people we expected."
The genre is structurally alive but institutionally underserved. Veterans and
gatekeepers sometimes echo the obituary themselves — an uncomfortable internal
contradiction.

📚 **Structural reality:** Rap Kreyòl exists on a spectrum:
- Artists who sustain themselves financially through music (Kolonel Freeze,
  King Street, Topson)
- Artists releasing consistently with limited institutional support (Fantom,
  Socrate Ankor)
- New artists uploading daily to Sajes Net Ale and similar platforms
The content is there. The machinery to amplify it is inconsistent.

📚 **Rap Kreyòl vs. Konpa — different registers:**
Where Konpa celebrates love, aspiration, and communal joy, Rap Kreyòl tends
toward social critique, street reality, and political commentary. Toby Anbakè
described his album as "unfiltered honesty and lyrical dexterity." This is the
genre where the crisis in Haiti surfaces most directly in popular music.

📚 **International reach:** Topson performs at AfroPiano Fest — Houston (May),
Milan (June), London (August), Accra (December). Rap Kreyòl is reaching global
Afrobeats/world music circuits, not just diaspora venues.

📚 **Digital distribution as equalizer:** iTunes Hip-Hop/Rap chart placements
(Toby Anbakè at #9 and #22) demonstrate that Rap Kreyòl can reach mainstream
digital charts when the release quality and marketing are there.

---

## 26.67 — Key Rap Kreyòl Artists and Infrastructure

📚 **Toby Anbakè**
Double album: "Nothing to Lose" (11 tracks, 41 min) + "Still Nothing to Love"
(9 tracks, 29 min), released August 15, 2025. iTunes Hip-Hop/Rap chart: #9 and
#22 respectively. Klēb K-Fé called it "the most important rap album of this year."
Key single: "Reallionnaire" (pre-release, built momentum via X Spaces and comment
sections). Features across both albums: Zile, Freedom, MechansT, Vanessa Désiré,
Watson G, Troubleboy Hitmaket, Guarionex. Described as uniting the "factions" of
the rap kreyòl community — a deeply divided scene given one release of this scale.

📚 **Ded Kra-Z**
Album "Yo Di M Nan Dyab" ("They Tell Me I'm a Devil/Troublemaker" roughly).
Featured on The Shade Room (29M+ Instagram followers) — one of the largest
U.S. pop culture platforms. Crossover visibility for Rap Kreyòl to a mainstream
American audience.

📚 **Topson**
Signed to Villon (label). Has brand deals. International performance circuit:
AfroPiano Fest across four continents. One of the most commercially positioned
Rap Kreyòl artists. Represents the genre's potential at the next level.

📚 **Kolonel Freeze**
Veteran. Relocated to United States. Sustains himself financially through music.

📚 **King Street** — lives off his art. Active.

📚 **Fantom** — "Pioneer and godfather" of Rap Kreyòl. Releases consistently.
Industry recognition without proportional commercial machinery behind him.

📚 **Socrate Ankor** — EP "Konsyan" (recent release, 2025).

📚 **Roody Roodboy** — "Tou 9" (16 songs, 56 min, August 2023). Active.

📚 **MasterBrain** — "10 Is It" (11 songs, 41 min, November 2024).

📚 **Kenny** — "Time To Shine" (12 songs, 36 min, September 2024). Described
in a headline as potentially reaching arena-level: "Is Kenny Haiti Next?"

📚 **Rap Kreyòl media infrastructure:**

| Platform/Person | Role |
|---|---|
| *Sajes Net Ale* | Platform for new Haitian rap releases — daily uploads |
| Obenson Hip Hop Street | Rap Kreyòl show on TikTok — keeps conversations alive |
| RBens | Podcast dissecting the genre with depth |
| Gandhi | Promised new rap kreyòl show (2025) |

📚 These are described as "culture workers filling the void left by more prominent
outlets that have increasingly turned their backs on the genre." The infrastructure
for Rap Kreyòl is grassroots and dispersed — not institutionally centered.

---

## 26.68 — Guy Wewe Radio A: The Music Media Gatekeeper

📚 **Guy Wewe Radio A** is led by **Guy Webern Guerrier** and is described as
"one of the top three media institutions" in Haitian music. For artists, appearing
there is "a rite of passage" — a career-defining moment.

📚 **Scale (as of June 2025):**
- YouTube: 888,000 subscribers, 250M+ total views
- Instagram: 725,000 followers
- TikTok: 253,000 followers
- X: 86,000 followers
- SoundCloud: 15,000 followers
- Estimated annual revenue: ~$350,000 (Klēb K-Fé estimate)

📚 **Function in the industry:** "If you're releasing an album, a single, or
even an artistic pivot, the first stop should be Guy Wewe Radio A." Every major
release comes with a "Guy Wewe moment" — an interview, a debut, a sneak peek.
This is "promotional currency backed by cultural capital."

📚 **The studio as cultural space:** Artists describe walking in as "entering a
sanctuary of creativity — both professional and personal." The atmosphere signals
"your art matters." This is not just promotion — it is validation.

📚 **The gatekeeper dynamic:** A nod from Guy Wewe "means doors swing wide open."
This is a concentration of cultural power in one institution and one person. In
an industry where distribution is fragmented, he is the centralizing force.

📚 **Persona relevance — Kenzy and Jèftè:** Guy Wewe Radio A's TikTok presence
(253K followers) means his content reaches the platforms where Kenzy and Jèftè
consume music news. A "Guy Wewe moment" for an artist is likely to circulate as
a koupe lage fragment through their feeds.

---

## 26.69 — Female Voices in Haitian Media and Music

📚 **KitKat Ayitibiyografi** (Catherine Desmarattes) — media personality described
as "reshaping mainstream media for Haitian women" with "fearless authenticity."
A named force in Haitian media, not just music. Indicates a growing female media
presence alongside the music industry's female voices (Fatima, Rebecca Zama,
Vanessa Désiré, Dumornay in football).

📚 **Fatima** — "Pwoteje M" (Protect Me) at #2 on the worldwide chart. The album
title is spiritually resonant — protection as a value, a prayer, a statement.
Her chart position at #2 alongside Klass at #1 confirms women are competing at
the top of the Konpa market.

📚 **Vanessa Désiré** — featured on Toby Anbakè's Rap Kreyòl album, adding
"emotional depth." Female voices appearing in the rap kreyòl space.

📚 **Structural note:** Women appear as artists (Fatima, Rebecca Zama), as
media figures (KitKat), and as collaborators (Vanessa Désiré). The Haitian music
and media space is not male-only, though Konpa is historically male-band dominated.

---

## 26.70 — Film: "July 7: Who Killed the President of Haiti?"

📚 **Director: Robenson Lauvince** — Haitian filmmaker based in Boston.
**City of Boston honored him** (November 2025) for his feature film
"July 7: Who Killed the President of Haiti?" — about the assassination of
President Jovenel Moïse.

📚 The film is described as "pushing Caribbean stories onto the global stage."
It confirms: the Jovenel assassination (already in the Jumo corpus as a core
political event) has entered the cultural/artistic domain. Haitian artists are
processing the assassination creatively, not just politically.

📚 **Boston-Haiti creative link:** A Haitian filmmaker, Boston-based, being
honored by the City of Boston for work about Haiti's defining political trauma.
This intersection (diaspora, Boston, political history, creative work) is
relevant to Sophonie's persona (Miami diaspora) and to the broader diaspora
experience of processing Haiti from abroad through art.

---

## 26.71 — "Peyi a Se Lakay" — Political Accountability Law (February 2026)

📚 **"Peyi a Se Lakay"** — "The Country Is Home."
A new law or political proposal (February 2026, covered under Community +
Political Klēb on Klēb K-Fé): *If you govern Haiti, your family should share
the same conditions as the people, right here at home.*

📚 This is an accountability measure targeting the political class: leaders
whose families live abroad (in Miami, New York, Paris) while Haiti deteriorates.
The requirement: your family stays home, in the same conditions as everyone else.

📚 **Why this matters for the Jumo corpus:** This intersects directly with
the existing *ensekirite a gen mèt* thesis and the *gro poisson yo* framework.
The political class that controls insecurity and extracts from Haiti has a specific
mobility pattern — they and their families exit when things get bad. "Peyi a Se
Lakay" names that pattern as unacceptable and proposes structural accountability.

📚 **Kenzy's likely register:** This is exactly the kind of political moment Kenzy
would engage with — either as a serious accountability demand or as teyat
("they'll pass it and ignore it like everything else"). His register on this
specific law needs validation.

---

<!-- ═══ Jumo_Corpus_Section26_Health_Addition.md ═══ -->

## Sources: MSPP (mspp.gouv.ht) · MSF (msf.org) · PAHO/WHO · Haitian Times · Gazette Univ

---

---

## 26.74 — MSPP: Institutional Profile

📚 The **Ministère de la Santé Publique et de la Population (MSPP)** is Haiti's
national health authority. It defines and implements national public health policy,
coordinates health services across the territory, regulates medicines and providers,
and manages epidemiological surveillance and emergency response.

📚 **Current leadership (as of January 2025):**
- Minister: **Dr. Bertrand Sinal**
- Directeur Général: **Dr. Lauré Adrien**

📚 **Structural organization:**
- 10 **Directions Sanitaires Départementales (DSD)** — one per department,
  responsible for local execution
- Central technical directorates: Laboratoire National de Santé Publique,
  Centre Ambulancier National
- Official digital services: health exam results portal, professional license
  verification, accredited training institutions list, personnel management platform

📚 **Twitter/X:** @msppofficiel

📚 **National Health Week (April 11, 2026):** MSPP coordinated nationwide
activities including breast cancer screening, non-communicable disease awareness,
pest control (dératisation and fumigation), and collective physical activities —
in partnership with the Youth/Sports Ministry (MJSAC) and Women's Rights Ministry
(MCFDF). A health fair was held in presence of the Secretary General of the
Primature and PAHO/WHO representation.

📚 **Historic institutional recognition:** Haiti was elected to the **WHO Executive
Council** (confirmed June 2025) — described as "recognition of Haiti's growing role
on the global health stage."

---

## 26.75 — Healthcare System Collapse: The Numbers

📚 **This is the most concentrated set of facts in the entire corpus for understanding
the health environment in which Marilène, Marie-Ange, and Jèftè live.**

📚 **Port-au-Prince hospital collapse (January–June 2025):**
- **60–80% of Port-au-Prince health facilities are closed or non-functional**
  due to gang violence — confirmed by MSF reporting, October 2025
- Approximately **30 hospitals** have ceased operations following armed attacks,
  fires, or vandalism since 2022
- Among closed major facilities: **Hôpital Universitaire d'État d'Haïti (HUEH)**
  — the country's largest public hospital — closed
- **Bernard Mevs Hospital** — treated wounded police officers — closed
- **University Hospital of Mirebalais** — referenced as impacted

📚 **The one remaining major public hospital in Port-au-Prince:**
**Hôpital universitaire de la Paix** — the only major public hospital still
functioning in the capital as of mid-2025. It is "regularly overwhelmed."
This is the hospital referenced in Marilène's existing system prompt as having
received Citadelle stampede victims without power for a decade.

📚 **Population health need:**
- **2 in 5 Haitians** (approximately 40%) are in urgent need of medical care
  due to widespread insecurity and violence — MSF figure
- Between January–June 2025 alone, MSF treated at MSF facilities only:
  - 13,300 patients admitted to emergency rooms
  - 2,267 victims of violence
  - 2,600 victims and survivors of sexual violence
  - **26% of violence victims are minors** — up from 11% in 2024
  - Most minors under age 15; one third are girls

📚 **Violence mortality:**
- **16,000+ people killed** in armed violence since start of 2022 — UN figure
- **H1 2025:** 3,100 deaths + 1,100 injuries
- **2024:** 5,600 killings — a 20% increase from 2023
- **1.4 million displaced** since 2022

📚 **Restricted movement and healthcare access:**
The closure of hospitals is compounded by restricted movement. Gang territorial
control means people cannot reach the hospitals that remain open. The healthcare
collapse is not only about closed facilities — it is about the inability to travel
to functioning ones. This is the structural reality Jèftè lives when he avoids
health facilities (route crosses gang territory).

---

## 26.76 — MSF: From Lifeline to Forced Withdrawal

📚 MSF (Médecins Sans Frontières / Doctors Without Borders) has been operating in
Haiti since the 1990s. For much of the crisis period (2021–2025), MSF facilities
functioned as the primary emergency care infrastructure for Port-au-Prince when
public hospitals collapsed. Their partial withdrawal is not an NGO story — it is
a healthcare infrastructure story.

📚 **Permanent closure of Turgeau Emergency Center (October 14–15, 2025):**
The MSF Emergency and Referral Center in Turgeau, Port-au-Prince, was permanently
closed after escalating gang violence made operations impossible.

**Timeline:**
- Originally opened 2006 in Martissant
- Relocated to Turgeau in 2021 — Martissant had already become too dangerous
- 2021–March 2025: treated **100,000+ patients**. Employed 206 people.
- November 11, 2024: MSF ambulance hijacked in Delmas — patient killed, staff threatened
- November 22, 2024: All Port-au-Prince operations suspended ~3 weeks
- March 15, 2025: MSF convoy attacked between Turgeau and Carrefour — operations suspended
- March–October 2025: Multiple ballistic assessments — no security solution found
- October 14–15, 2025: **Permanent closure announced**

📚 **Why permanent:** Building hit multiple times by stray bullets. Located in
immediate vicinity of active combat zones. No viable protection solution identified.
MSF awaiting a humanitarian corridor MOU between Port-au-Prince and Carrefour as
a prerequisite for considering any resumption of activities there.

📚 **Attack pattern against MSF specifically:**
- July 2023: 20 armed men entered MSF Tabarre hospital, forcibly removed a patient
- December 2023: Patient executed after being taken from MSF ambulance in Turgeau
- November 2024: Ambulance hijacked, patient killed
- March 2025: Convoy attacked, triggering final suspension

📚 **Pattern significance:** Medical facilities and ambulances are not protected.
Medical neutrality does not function in Haiti's conflict environment. MSF — one
of the most operationally experienced humanitarian organizations in the world —
concluded that risks to patients and staff were unmanageable. This is not a
statement about MSF's commitment. It is a statement about conditions on the ground.

---

## 26.77 — What Remains: Functioning Healthcare Infrastructure

📚 **As of late 2025 / early 2026, the functioning emergency healthcare
infrastructure in Port-au-Prince is:**

**MSF (still operational):**
- **Tabarre hospital** — trauma care, capacity increased 50% due to demand.
  26% of trauma cases linked to violence.
- **Cité Soleil emergency center** — still operational (in Jèftè's commune)
- **Pran Men'm clinic** — survivors of sexual violence
- **Primary health care:** Delmas 4, Bel Air, Bas Bel Air/La Saline
- **Isaïe Jeanty maternity hospital** — reopened in MSPP partnership (Oct 2025)

**Public sector:**
- **Hôpital universitaire de la Paix** — only functioning major public hospital
  in Port-au-Prince. Regularly overwhelmed.
- **Hôpital Justinien** (Cap-Haïtien) — revitalized per MSPP June 2025 report
- **Hôpital La Providence** (Gonaïves) — revitalized per MSPP
- **Jérémie** — neurologie service reinforced (specialized care advance)
- **10 DSD structures** (departmental) — operating at varying levels nationally

📚 **The Cité Soleil MSF emergency center as a specific Jèftè detail:**
MSF maintains an emergency center in Cité Soleil. This means there is functioning
emergency care in Jèftè's commune — but accessing it still requires navigating gang
territorial logic. The center's existence does not eliminate the security calculation;
it changes the math of it.

📚 **Community health workers as the last functional layer:**
With hospitals closed and MSF partially withdrawn, community health workers
(Ajans Sante Kominotè) like Marilène become structurally more important — and
structurally more isolated. They are the last remaining touchpoint between
communities and the health system in many areas. Their workload has increased as
formal facilities have closed. Their institutional support has not.

---

## 26.78 — PAHO/WHO Country Cooperation Strategy 2026–2028

📚 On **October 1, 2025**, MSPP Minister Dr. Bertrand Sinal and PAHO Director
Dr. Jarbas Barbosa signed Haiti's **first-ever Country Cooperation Strategy (CCS)**,
covering 2026–2028. Signed at PAHO headquarters in Washington, D.C.

📚 **Three strategic priorities:**
1. Strengthen the health system and ensure universal access to quality care
   regardless of financial means
2. Improve prevention, control, and management of major health issues
3. Strengthen preparedness and response to health emergencies — using the
   **NEXUS approach**: connecting humanitarian aid, development, and peacebuilding

📚 **The NEXUS approach** explicitly acknowledges that Haiti's health emergency
cannot be separated from security and governance. The framework recognizes that
"humanitarian aid, development, and peacebuilding" must be linked — not sequential.

📚 **What this means in practice vs. on paper:**
The strategy was signed while 60–80% of Port-au-Prince hospitals were closed and
MSF was permanently closing Turgeau. The gap between the diplomatic language
("first cooperation framework of its kind") and the operational reality is extreme.
This is institutional documentation of aspiration against a backdrop of collapse.

📚 **Strategic plans also in development:**
- **Plan stratégique 2025–2029** for non-communicable diseases and trauma prevention
  (validated July 2025 in national workshop with MSPP, PAHO/WHO, civil society)
- Cancer cervical: 10 thermo-coagulation devices delivered to MSPP
- Respiratory disease pandemic preparedness plan: national framework developed

---

## 26.79 — MSPP Human Resources Reform

📚 **The chronic structural problem:** Insufficient qualified health personnel,
particularly in rural and peri-urban areas. This is a decades-old problem that
the current crisis has accelerated.

📚 **Recent MSPP actions (12-month period, reported January 2026):**
- **1,708 nominations effective** out of 2,646 projected — across the national
  health territory
- **233 infirmières (nurses) recruited** through a national competitive examination —
  described as professionalizing and rationalizing public sector health recruitment

📚 **Permanent departure of qualified staff:**
The closure of hospitals due to violence has caused a parallel exodus of medical
professionals. Doctors and nurses leave insecure areas, emigrate, or relocate.
The MSPP nomination figures represent an attempt to replace staff who have left —
not an expansion of an existing workforce.

📚 **Budget line for health (2025–2026):** A line item was included in the national
budget for the health sector — confirmed by Conseiller-Président Gilles, responsible
for health within the Conseil Présidentiel de Transition (CPT): "The great battle
we must fight is to preserve the dignity of the people."

---

## 26.80 — Cholera: Ongoing Endemic Presence

📚 **Cholera remains an active public health crisis** in Haiti. The MSPP publishes
weekly Situation Reports (SITREP) on cholera. As of early 2025, weekly SITREPs
were active through at least week 10 of 2025 (confirmed on MSPP documentation page).

📚 **Context:** Cholera was reintroduced to Haiti after the 2010 earthquake (by UN
peacekeeping troops, later acknowledged). It has never been fully eliminated. The
current insecurity and displacement crisis creates ideal conditions for persistence:
disrupted water systems, overcrowded displacement camps, collapsed health facilities
unable to manage cases.

📚 **For Marilène:** Cholera case identification, community referral, and household
water safety messaging are likely part of her CHW responsibilities. The weekly SITREP
cycle means this is an ongoing operational context, not a historical one.

📚 **For Granmoun Lisette:** Artibonite department has historically been a cholera
epicenter (the Artibonite River was the source of the 2010 outbreak). Rural Artibonite
households without running water face ongoing cholera risk.

---

## 26.81 — The Psychological Health Layer

📚 **From a Haitian academic/psychiatric source (referenced in MSPP/Gazette Univ
search results):** An analysis of "collective psychological fragility" in Haiti,
described as linked to historical traumas, catastrophes, violence, and calling for
"global reconstruction." This is academic language for what the corpus already names
in human terms through personas.

📚 **The MSF figure that requires special note:**
Between January and June 2025, MSF treated **2,600 victims and survivors of sexual
violence** across Port-au-Prince facilities. The annual extrapolation (5,200+) means
that sexual violence is not a peripheral crisis — it is a central and ongoing public
health emergency. The Madan Sara vulnerability documented in Marie-Ange's corpus
section (rape and theft as named occupational risks) is confirmed at systemic scale
by MSF clinical data.

📚 **Children as victims:**
26% of violence victims at MSF facilities are minors. Most under 15. One third
girls. This is not the background of a health system. This is the daily intake
of a functional emergency department in Port-au-Prince in 2025.

---

   de la Paix (overwhelmed) or an MSF primary care site in her area?

🔲 Has he ever used it, or does the security calculation still prevent it?

   for emergency transport if he or family needed it?

**Granmoun Lisette — Artibonite health context:**
🔲 Is cholera a present household concern in her commune?
🔲 Does she use the local dispensary for referrals or has it closed/degraded?
🔲 Has she lost access to any health services she previously used?

**Across all personas:**
🔲 Is the hospital closure situation known and named in daily conversation,
   or is it experienced as invisible degradation?
🔲 Does anyone in the persona set use the phrase "lopital fèmen" (hospital
   closed) as a shorthand for Haiti's general dysfunction?
🔲 Is MSF recognized by name among non-elite personas — or known only as
   "the foreign doctors" or another informal reference?

---

## KEY FINDING FOR JUMO RESEARCH SIMULATIONS

📚 **The Pilplan misconception already in Marilène's system prompt** — young
men taking women's oral contraceptives believing it prevents pregnancy — now sits
inside a confirmed context: 2,600 sexual violence survivors treated by MSF in H1
2025 alone, 26% of violence victims are children, the only major public hospital
is overwhelmed. The Pilplan error is not an isolated health literacy gap. It is
one behavior in a healthcare desert where clinical knowledge barely reaches
communities and misinformation fills the vacuum.

📚 **The gap between MSPP's language and the ground is total.**
"Universal access to quality care" (CCS 2026–2028) was signed the same month
MSF permanently closed Turgeau. The institutional aspiration and the operational
reality are in complete opposition. This is the environment Marilène works in:
she navigates between frameworks that sound like coverage and communities that
have no functional hospital in reach.

---

## Sources: UNOPS (unops.org) · UNSDG · Flash Haiti · World Bank project data

## 26.83 — UNOPS: Profile in Haiti

📚 **UNOPS (United Nations Office for Project Services)** has operated in Haiti
for over 20 years. It is not a traditional aid organization — it is a UN service
delivery agency: it provides project management, procurement, infrastructure,
logistics, and technical services that enable other actors (governments, NGOs,
UN agencies, donors) to implement their programs.

📚 **What UNOPS provides that others cannot:**
- Large-scale procurement and contracting with fiduciary accountability
- Project management in complex, insecure, post-disaster contexts
- Infrastructure design and construction to technical standards
- Local capacity building embedded in every project

📚 **Haiti portfolio sectors:** Health, education, transport/roads, energy,
environment, food security, disaster risk reduction, housing.

📚 **Primary funder:** World Bank (multiple projects). Also: Brazil, Cuba,
Canada, Mexico, PAHO, UNDP, UNFPA, Petunia Foundation, MINUSTAH (historical).

📚 **Haiti office:** Pétion-Ville, Port-au-Prince 8228. Phone: 509 2816-0908.

📚 **For the Jumo corpus — why UNOPS matters:**
UNOPS is the operational backbone behind many infrastructure projects that
Haitians experience but never attribute to UNOPS. When a road gets repaired in
Artibonite, a hospital gets solar panels, or irrigation canals get rehabilitated in
Nord-Ouest — UNOPS is often the implementing agency. The gap between
"UNOPS implemented this" and what Haitians know about why it happened or who
did it is the NGO economy visibility problem the corpus already documents.

---

## 26.84 — Food Security: The Scale of the Crisis

📚 **5.4 million people in Haiti face acute food insecurity** — confirmed by
UNOPS project documentation (2024-2025 data).

📚 **Geography of hunger:**
Over **75% of food-insecure Haitians live in rural areas.** For Dieunel
(Plateau Central) and Granmoun Lisette (Artibonite), food insecurity is not
a crisis they observe — it is the structural condition of the people around them.
Agriculture is not a lifestyle choice in these contexts. It is the only survival
mechanism available.

📚 **Root causes confirmed by UNOPS:**
- Poor infrastructure (roads, irrigation, storage)
- Recurrent droughts and seismic events
- Persistent insecurity and political instability
- Economic instability
- Climate shocks — particularly hurricane-driven destruction
The crisis is multi-causal and compound. Each shock (hurricane, earthquake,
gang territorial expansion) disrupts systems that were already fragile.

📚 **Northwest Haiti as food insecurity epicenter:**
The Nord-Ouest (northwest) department is described as one of the highest levels
of food insecurity in the world — despite having fertile lands and underground
water resources. The problem is not soil. It is infrastructure, insecurity, and
the absence of reliable water access for agriculture.

---

## 26.85 — PARSA: Emergency Resilient Agriculture for Food Security

📚 **Project name:** Emergency Resilient Agriculture for Food Security (PARSA)
**Funder:** World Bank — $5 million
**Implementing agency:** UNOPS
**Government partner:** MARNDR (Ministère de l'Agriculture, des Ressources
Naturelles et du Développement Rural)
**Location:** Nord-Ouest department (four locations)
**Status:** Extension phase underway as of December 2025

📚 **What the project does:**
- Rehabilitation of damaged irrigation canals
- Retaining walls and water distribution systems
- Installation of pumping stations
- Rehabilitation of over **15 kilometers of rural roads**
- Land management measures (soil protection, cultivation improvement)
- Designs for aqueducts and hybrid energy systems
- Capacity building for irrigators' associations (governance, technical, organizational)

📚 **Results to date (as of late 2025):**
- **21,000+ workdays** generated for nearly **800 participants**
- **40% women** among participants — deliberate inclusion target
- **962 plots across 419 hectares** mapped in comprehensive parcel mapping exercise
- **90 participants** trained in digital mapping/GIS, construction, water management,
  and logistics

📚 **Worker voice (Willianise Charles, participant):**
The works represent "a significant step forward for the development of the community.
These infrastructures improve access and safety, they improve the lives of the
populations."

📚 **What PARSA represents for the corpus:**
This is the development project as it actually functions in rural Haiti — not a
handout, but temporary employment and infrastructure that leaves something behind.
The 40% women target acknowledges that rural women are doing the agricultural work.
The 21,000 workdays created are individual households with income for that period.
The 962 parcel maps represent land tenure clarity in a country where land disputes
are one of the primary triggers for Vodou-as-justice consultations (documented
elsewhere in corpus).

---

## 26.86 — Road Infrastructure: The Connectivity Problem

📚 **Haiti relies heavily on road transport, yet its network faces poor
accessibility and persistent maintenance failures.** During the rainy season,
rural communities are regularly cut off from main roads — losing access to
healthcare, education, and markets.

📚 **Saint-Michel de L'Attalaye Road Maintenance Centre (Artibonite):**
New center constructed in Artibonite department. Supports daily maintenance
and operation of road infrastructure in the region. Includes training of
local authorities in road maintenance. Training targeted primarily at women
and young people — employability focus.

📚 **Grand'Anse and Sud departments:**
Roads damaged by Hurricane Matthew (2016) built and rehabilitated. Completed
as of UNOPS reporting. Matthew roads remained damaged for years before repair —
illustrating the timeline of recovery in Haiti.

📚 **World Bank road infrastructure project (national):**
Ongoing project strengthening critical road infrastructure across the country
in UNOPS partnership with the government. Scale and specific departments not
specified in sources reviewed.

📚 **For Marie-Ange specifically:**
Road quality is not abstract for a Madan Sara who travels on camions through
mountain roads to move produce. A road rehabilitation project in her operating
zone is the difference between getting to market and not getting there. The
infrastructure Marie-Ange depends on — and that can be blocked by gangs — is
the same infrastructure UNOPS is trying to maintain and improve.

📚 **For Dieunel (Plateau Central):**
Rural road quality determines whether Plateau Central farmers can access
markets. Connectivity to Artibonite or Port-au-Prince markets is infrastructure-
dependent. A new road maintenance center in Artibonite does not directly serve
Plateau Central but reflects the broader investment pattern.

---

## 26.87 — Hospital Energy: Solar Systems for 5 Facilities

📚 **UNOPS is partnering with the World Bank** to improve energy access and
reduce reliance on fossil fuels at **five hospitals across Haiti** through
hybrid solar energy systems.

📚 **Why this matters:** The corpus already documents that a hospital received
the Citadelle stampede victims with no power for a decade (in Marilène's system
prompt). Hospital energy failure is not exceptional — it is structural. Generators
require fuel. Fuel requires supply chains. Supply chains are blocked by insecurity.
Solar systems bypass that vulnerability.

📚 **Broader health infrastructure construction:**
Since 2014, UNOPS (with UNDP, UNFPA, and multiple government donors) has built
**11 health facilities**. One documented result: a hospital in an unnamed location
went from **30 patients/day to 120–150 consultations/day** after construction.
This is the healthcare demand that was there all along — unmet until a facility
existed.

📚 **The gap this reveals:**
UNOPS has built 11 health facilities and is solarizing 5 hospitals. MSF has
permanently closed the Turgeau emergency center and approximately 30 hospitals
have ceased operations. The construction is real. The destruction is faster.

---

## 26.88 — Hurricane Melissa: New Disaster Entry

📚 **Hurricane Melissa** is referenced in UNOPS project documentation (late 2025)
as a recent major natural disaster that "battered the Caribbean nation, deepening
its mounting insecurity, destroying lives and livelihoods, damaging critical
infrastructure and worsening the food crisis."

📚 Hurricane Melissa is also referenced in PAHO documentation on respiratory
disease pandemic preparedness. The hurricane triggered health infrastructure
damage and population displacement on top of the existing security crisis.

📚 **This is a new event not previously in the Jumo corpus.**
The 2021 earthquake (August, southern Haiti) and Hurricane Matthew (2016) are
historical references in the corpus. Melissa appears to be a more recent event —
exact date not confirmed in sources reviewed, but referenced as current context
in late 2025 UNOPS and PAHO material.

🔲 **Validation required:** When exactly did Hurricane Melissa hit? Which
departments were most affected? How does it register in community speech for
each persona in its affected zones?

---

## 26.89 — The UN System in Haiti: 28 Agencies

📚 As of the current UN Cooperation Framework (January 2023–December 2028),
**28 UN agencies operate in Haiti.** The framework is the UNDAF/United Nations
Sustainable Development Cooperation Framework (CF).

📚 **Key agency country leads:**
| Agency | Role | Lead |
|---|---|---|
| UNOPS | Infrastructure/procurement | Dabagai Dabagai |
| WFP | Food | Wanja Kaaria (Country Director) |
| WHO/PAHO | Health | Oscar Barreneche |
| World Bank | Financing | Anne-Lucie Lefebvre |
| UNDP | Development | — |
| UNFPA | Population/reproductive health | — |
| UNV | Volunteers | Darline Monfort |

📚 **The 28-agency figure is significant for the Jumo corpus.**
The NGO economy section (already in the corpus) documents Haiti's dependence on
external actors. 28 UN agencies — each with staff, offices, vehicles, salaries,
procurement — represents a parallel economy operating alongside Haiti's formal and
informal economies. This is Nadège's professional world (NGO/dual world persona).

📚 **UNDAF framework 2023–2028:**
The cooperation framework covers exactly the period of Jumo's pilot deployment
(2026 onward). The institutional architecture being built around Haiti's recovery
is the context Marilène, Nadège, and program designers working with Jumo clients
are navigating.

---

All 🔲 — require Dulex validation before use in persona voice.

   (World Bank) in how she discusses her work?

   inertia in her daily professional life?

   (cash-for-work, irrigation rehabilitation)?

   left behind?

🔲 Has the Saint-Michel road maintenance center or any road rehabilitation project
   affected her commune's connectivity?
🔲 Does she experience NGO/UN projects as outsiders arriving and leaving, or as
   something that leaves permanent change?
🔲 Does she use the phrase "yo vin fè travay" (they came to do work) to describe
   all such projects regardless of their actual permanence?

**Marie-Ange — road quality and market access:**

🔲 Is road rehabilitation by an outside agency experienced as government action,
   NGO action, or something indistinguishable?

**Hurricane Melissa:**
🔲 When did it hit? Which departments? How does it register in community speech?

**General NGO economy:**
🔲 Is "World Bank" (Banque Mondiale) a recognized term in non-elite personas'
   vocabulary, or is it an abstraction?
🔲 Does "yo vin" (they came) serve as the generic verb for any external project,
   regardless of source?
🔲 Is there a specific Kreyòl phrase for the cycle of NGO arrival, work, and
   departure that communities have named?

---

## KEY STRUCTURAL FINDING

📚 **The 5.4 million food-insecure, the 75% rural concentration, and the 21,000
PARSA workdays are in direct tension with each other.**

21,000 workdays for 800 participants in one project addresses a fraction of a
5.4 million-person crisis. This is not a criticism of PARSA — it is the structural
math of humanitarian response in Haiti. Every project that works is real and
meaningful. None of them, individually or collectively, closes the gap between
the scale of need and the scale of response.

This is the context in which Granmoun Lisette watches development organizations
arrive and leave. In which Dieunel manages a kitchen garden and depends on
remittances because agriculture alone cannot sustain the household. In which
Marie-Ange travels on camion roads that may or may not be passable.
*"Pa jete chodyè ize pou chodyè nèf"* — the old pot the NGO replaced may have
been broken, but at least it was theirs.

---

*Sources: UNOPS (unops.org), UNSDG (unsdg.un.org), Flash Haiti, World Bank project data*


<!-- ═══ Jumo_Corpus_Section26_OperationUpholdDemocracy.md ═══ -->

---

## 26.91 — Operation Uphold Democracy (1994): Mechanics and Timeline

📚 **Operation Uphold Democracy** was the September 1994 US-led multinational
military intervention that restored President Jean-Bertrand Aristide to power
after the 1991 coup led by Lieutenant General Raoul Cédras.

📚 **Authorization:** UN Security Council Resolution 940 (July 31, 1994) —
authorized "all necessary means" to restore democracy in Haiti.

📚 **Scale:** Nearly 25,000 US military personnel from all branches. Coalition
included Poland, Argentina, Jamaica, and several Caribbean nations. Led by
Lieutenant General Hugh Shelton, Joint Task Force 120.

📚 **Deployment vessels:** USS America and USS Dwight D. Eisenhower carried
the first brigade of the 10th Mountain Division.

📚 **The last-minute diplomatic turn:**
The operation was initially planned as a forced military entry, with extensive
preparation for armed resistance from Haitian military and paramilitary forces.
Hours before the landing, a diplomatic mission led by **former President Jimmy
Carter, Senator Sam Nunn, and General Colin Powell** negotiated a peaceful entry
with the Cédras government. The invasion plans were adapted in real time from
hostile entry to permissive entry — a significant operational pivot.

📚 **Psychological operations:**
Between September 13–17, 1994, the US military distributed approximately
**7 million leaflets** over major Haitian cities, promoting themes of "democracy,
prosperity, and lawfulness." Radio broadcasts accompanied the leaflet drops.
This was the largest pre-landing information operation of the mission.

📚 **Execution (September 19, 1994):**
US forces adopted a posture of "confident authority." The goal was visible control
with civil demeanor — not occupation. Cédras transferred power to Major General
Jean-Claude Duperval. Special Forces conducted patrols, disarmed hostile forces,
and secured key areas. Markets were reopened. A new Haitian police force was
established.

📚 **Transition timeline:**
- March 1995: Operation handed over to **UNMIH** (UN Mission in Haiti)
- 1996: US troops departed Haiti

📚 **Official conclusion:**
The Clinton administration declared the operation a success. The long-term
stability of Haiti was acknowledged as a remaining challenge from the moment
US troops left. "Operation Uphold Democracy concluded… it also underscored the
complexities of nation building and the need for sustained international support."

---

## 26.92 — The Pattern This Established

📚 **Operation Uphold Democracy is the first in a sequence** that the corpus
already documents: UNMIH (1995) → MINUSTAH (2004–2017, 14 missions) →
Binational Security Support Mission / MSS (2024–present, Sri Lankan troops
in Artibonite).

📚 The 1994 intervention set the template:
- External force restores order
- Transition to UN mission
- UN mission transitions to Haitian institutions
- Haitian institutions fail to hold
- New crisis → new external intervention

📚 **Each cycle has its own documented harm:**
The MINUSTAH cholera introduction (2010, UN peacekeepers from Nepal) is the
most severe. Granmoun Lisette's corpus entry already references the 14 prior
missions. Her frame — "I know what the last 14 did" — is the accumulated reading
of this 30-year cycle.

📚 **The 7 million leaflets are significant for the trust architecture.**
The US military's choice of leaflets and radio as influence tools in 1994 mirrors
what the corpus documents about contemporary information environments (koupe lage,
radio as primary information source in rural areas). External actors have historically
tried to shape Haitian public opinion through mass media interventions — and
Haitians have developed sophisticated filters for exactly this kind of messaging.

---

## 26.93 — The Robert Malval Quote: Handling Note

📚 **Quote from the documentary:**
Robert Malval — Prime Minister of Haiti under Aristide, early 1990s — told
US Special Envoy Lawrence Pezzullo: *"Larry, 99% of Haitians are paranoid,
and that includes me."* Malval added: "He was not saying this in a demeaning way
even to himself — the history made them that way. They have no faith in themselves,
in institutions, in their friends, or in anything that you might call the basis for
a civilized society."

📚 **Source context:** This quote is from a US diplomat's account, referenced in
a documentary. It is a 1990s political figure speaking to a US official about
institutional trust collapse. It is NOT a validated cultural informant statement
from inside the community.

📚 **Why it resonates with the corpus:**
The trust architecture already documented (the rusted cord, the door that hasn't
been knocked, "nou va wè") maps directly onto what Malval described. The history
that produced this distrust is the same history — the coups, the reign of terror,
5,000 Aristide supporters killed, the paramilitaries — that is in the political
history sections.

📚 **CRITICAL HANDLING NOTE:**
Do NOT use the Malval quote to frame Haitian character. The quote describes
a *result* of specific, named historical violence — not a cultural trait.
The word "paranoid" carries clinical and pejorative freight that should never
be applied to personas. The corpus reframes it as: *institutional trust was
systematically destroyed by documented, named, repeated violence.*
The Jumo frame is "rust on the cord" — not "paranoia." These are different
registers. One locates the cause in history. The other locates it in the person.
The Malval quote is corpus context for understanding the depth of the trust
collapse. It is never a persona description.

---

## Cross-Reference Updates

**Section on trust architecture** (Part One, Cultural Substrate):
The 1994 intervention's 7 million leaflets adds historical depth to why Haitians
are sophisticated readers of mass communication designed to shape behavior.
The leaflet operation was experienced by the grandparents of Kenzy and Jèftè.
Granmoun Lisette was alive for it. The pattern of external actors using media
to shape Haitian public opinion is 30+ years old.

**Section on MINUSTAH / foreign missions** (already in corpus):
Operation Uphold Democracy → UNMIH → MINUSTAH is now a complete chain.
The "15th mission" Sri Lankan troops Granmoun Lisette references now has
its origin point documented: 1994.

---

*Robert Malval quote: handle with care — corpus context only, never persona framing*

## 26.94 — Chokarella and Carel Pedre: Haiti's #1 Media Voice

📚 **Chokarella** (chokarella.com) is Haiti's dominant cultural media brand,
created by **Carel Pedre** — "to produce content to inform and inspire."
Tagline: "Your Portal to Haitian Culture, Music, and News."

📚 **Radio presence:**
- **Radio One Haiti, 90.1 MHz FM, Port-au-Prince**
- Worldwide streaming via chokarella.com
- **Flagship show: "Carel In The Morning"** — 6am to 10am daily
- Confirmed as **the #1 morning show in Haiti**
- Reaches **70% of the national territory** — homes and cars nationwide

📚 **Scale:** 480,611 Facebook likes. Active on Facebook, YouTube,
and through a dedicated platform (Chokarella Plus). Also has a live radio
web player on the main site.

📚 **What Chokarella covers:** Haitian music (Konpa, Rap Kreyòl, international
crossovers), culture (art, film, literature), news, sport, food, diaspora,
immigration, business ("Lundi Business"), and youth/digital media literacy.

📚 **Carel Pedre's role:** Not just a broadcaster but a cultural gatekeeper.
Features like "Carel In The Morning" function similarly to Guy Wewe Radio A
(music industry) — an artist or subject featured there reaches the widest
possible domestic audience. The show is the daily ambient sound of Haiti's
middle class and aspiring households.

📚 **For personas:**
- **Kenzy:** Almost certainly follows Chokarella on social media and knows
  Carel Pedre by name and voice.
- **Sophonie (Miami):** Likely streams Chokarella from the diaspora — it is
  explicitly available "all around the world."
- **Nadège:** Chokarella's Pétion-Ville register and cultural coverage aligns
  with her world.
- **Marie-Ange:** Radio One 90.1 reaches 70% of the territory — she may hear
  Carel in the morning while preparing for market, without naming the show.
- **Granmoun Lisette:** Radio reach in Artibonite — may or may not reach her
  commune specifically. 🔲 validate.
